    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8d93f46e2bdc44118e005322ff4fb3cc">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ed535377-008f-4e68-a8b4-1fa22c52fe8e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p class="author-name">Chun-Wei Huang</p>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science and Engineering, Feng Chia University, No. 100, Wenhwa Rd., Seatwen, Taichung, 40724 Taiwan</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Huang/Chun%E2%80%90Wei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Lo/Yu%E2%80%90Chieh" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yu-Chieh Lo</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Yu-Chieh Lo</p>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science and Engineering, National Yang Ming Chiao Tung University, Hsinchu, 30010 Taiwan</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Lo/Yu%E2%80%90Chieh">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Chang/Jeng%E2%80%90Kuei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Jeng-Kuei Chang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-name">Jeng-Kuei Chang</p>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science and Engineering, National Yang Ming Chiao Tung University, Hsinchu, 30010 Taiwan</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chang/Jeng%E2%80%90Kuei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wu/Wen%E2%80%90Wei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a10" data-db-target-for="a10" aria-controls="a10" aria-haspopup="true" id="a10_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Wen-Wei Wu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a10" aria-labelledby="a10_Ctrl" role="region" id="a10">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d92848033" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Recently, all-solid-state sodium batteries (Na-ASSBs) have received increased interest owing to their high safety and potential of high energy density. The potential of Na-ASSBs based on sodium superionic conductor (NASICON)-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(Na<sub>3</sub>VP) cathodes have been proven by their high capacity and a long cycling stability closely related to the microstructural evolution. However, the detailed kinetics of the electrochemical processes in the cathodes is still unclear. In this work, the sodiation/desodiation process of Na<sub>3</sub>VP&nbsp;is first investigated using in situ high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM). The intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Na<sub>2</sub>VP) phase with the P2<sub>1</sub>/c space group, which would be inhibited by constant electron beam irradiation, is observed at the atomic scale. With the calculated volume change and the electrode–electrolyte interface after cycling, it can be concluded that the &nbsp;Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase reduces the lattice mismatch between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NaVP), preventing structural collapse. Based on the density functional theory calculation (DFT), the Na<sup>+</sup> ion migrates more rapidly in the Na<sub>2</sub>VP structure, which facilitates the desodiation and sodiation processes. The formation of &nbsp;Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase lowers the formation energy of NaVP. This study demonstrates the dynamic evolution of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP structure, paving the way for an in-depth understanding of electrode materials for energy-storage applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6172-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6172-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Ever since Li<i><sub>x</sub></i>C<sub>6</sub>/organic electrolyte/Li<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>CoO<sub>2</sub> batteries were successfully commercialized by Sony Corporation in 1991, these lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the portable devices and consumer electronics market.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0001_R_d92847927e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Joule">1</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0002_R_d92847927e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the automotive sector, the development of LIBs with liquid electrolytes has stagnated owing to its flammable liquid electrolytes and insufficient energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0003_R_d92847927e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0004_R_d92847927e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ever-increasing demand for energy storage systems has invigorated the need to seek alternatives with higher performance, enhanced safety, and increased economic efficiency compared to conventional LIBs. In this respect, all-solid-state batteries (ASSBs) are regarded as the potential solution. Although the phenomenon occurring in a liquid electrolyte system and in a solid-state electrolyte system is identical, ASSBs can eliminate the safety concerns of traditional batteries by replacing flammable liquid electrolytes with solid electrolytes. Furthermore, solid electrolytes are more chemically and electrochemically stable than liquid electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0005_R_d92847927e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, batteries with solid-state design also show better adaptability to temperature changes, and have the potential of achieving both higher energy and power densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0006_R_d92847927e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ChemElectroChem">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Li has been the preferred in battery chemistries in recent decades. However, the cost of future LIBs remains uncertain due to the increasing demand of LIBs and uneven geographical distribution of Li.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0007_R_d92847927e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Joule">7</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0008_R_d92847927e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The search for alternative high-energy batteries using abundant and low-cost materials is imperative. Among the possible candidates, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) possess advantages such as abundant sodium supply, high energy storage capacity, and high electrochemical activity, which have attracted the attention of researchers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0009_R_d92847927e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>-<a href="#advs6172-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0014_R_d92847927e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Na<sub>3</sub>VP) is a promising electrode material, which crystallizes in a NASICON structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0015_R_d92847927e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy">15</a>-<a href="#advs6172-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0018_R_d92847927e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Nano">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It shows very promising capability in Na-ASSBs. Having the same structure as the highly conductive solid electrolyte Na<sub>3.4</sub>Zr<sub>2</sub>Si<sub>2.4</sub>P<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>12</sub> (NZSP), Na<sub>3</sub>VP showed excellent chemical compatibility with NZSP and great cycling stability when used as the positive electrode material.</p>
         
         <p>The structure of Na<sub>3</sub>VP is a 3D open framework composed of repeating lantern units, where two VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra are joined together by three corner-sharing PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. There are two different Na sites located in the framework: Na(1) and Na(2). The Na(1) sites are the octahedral sites between two VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, whereas the Na(2) sites are the tetrahedral sites between two adjacent PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra along the <i>c</i>-axis direction. The open diffusion paths and rigid structure in the polyanionic sublattice can be attributed to the strong covalent bonds between the oxygen atoms. The Na<sup>+</sup> ions can reversibly diffuse through the interstitial sites without a significant volume change<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0019_R_d92847927e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in the crystal structure, achieving high theoretical energy storage capacities and superior ionic mobility. Previous studies have demonstrated the high energy density, high rate, and long cycle life of Na<sub>3</sub>VP-based sodium-ion batteries, which are closely related to the Na<sub>3</sub>VP microstructure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0020_R_d92847927e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a>-<a href="#advs6172-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0022_R_d92847927e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the structural evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>VP during sodiation/desodiation remains ambiguous. Therefore, it is essential to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion and extraction mechanism of Na<sub>3</sub>VP.</p>
         
         <p>There have been growing interests in developing in situ techniques for battery studies, such as operando X-ray diffraction (XRD) technique<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0023_R_d92847927e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and in situ Raman methods.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0024_R_d92847927e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, the low strain of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F lattice with a volumetric variation of only 2.56% during Na intercalation/extraction processes was disclosed by operando XRD.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0025_R_d92847927e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In situ Raman analysis revealed a highly reversible three-phase transition as the sodium-ion storage mechanism of Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> during sodiation/desodiation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0026_R_d92847927e699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various in situ techniques, in situ TEM possesses the capability of resolving the microstructural evolution and a providing diffraction information of the electrode materials. It can create a variety of external fields, including electric and thermal fields, and can observe the dynamic structure evolutions at the same time. Based on the advantages mentioned above, in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) has also been utilized to observe various reaction phenomena, such as catalytic phenomena,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0027_R_d92847927e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun.">27</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0028_R_d92847927e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electromigration,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0029_R_d92847927e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Science">29</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0030_R_d92847927e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Acta Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and electrochemical reaction of batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0031_R_d92847927e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Mater. Today">31</a>-<a href="#advs6172-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0033_R_d92847927e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this work, an all-solid-state battery cell was prepared and the phase transition of Na<sub>3</sub>VP under charging/discharging was investigated using operando X-ray diffractometer (XRD) analysis. Moreover, the structural evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>VP during sodiation/desodiation process was observed via in situ high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) for the first time. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) images of Na<sub>3</sub>VP demonstrated the evolution of lattice spacing, verifying the Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/extraction during sodiation/desodiation process. Density functional theory (DFT) computations were employed to determine the formation energies of the cathode and the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusing in the cathode structure. In-depth information about the mechanism of the phase transition in the Na<sub>3</sub>VP–NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NaVP) system will be discussed in future research. This study unveils the structural evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>VP during the sodiation/desodiation process, providing fundamental information on Na<sub>3</sub>VP and laying the foundation for further research and future applications of Na-ASSBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6172-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6172-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> in the Supporting Information shows the as-synthesized all-solid-state Na<sub>3</sub>VP/NZSP sample. The XRD spectra of the sample showed that the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP were both indexed to the NASICON structure with the R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/caaaa746-68bd-4d0b-bbe5-84942fa09332/advs6172-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>c space group (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0034_R_d92847927e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small Methods">34</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0035_R_d92847927e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>Detailed information on the crystal structures of Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP is provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> in the Supporting Information. The microstructure of the sample was investigated with TEM. The interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP was continuous, indicating good contact between the solid-state electrode and electrolyte (as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0001">1a</a>). An enlarged TEM image of the interface is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> in the Supporting Information. The schematic diagram illustrates the sample comprising the Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode and NZSP electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0001">1b</a>). The HRTEM images and corresponding diffraction patterns showed that both Na<sub>3</sub>VP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0001">1c</a>) and NZSP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0001">1d</a>) were crystalline with a rhombohedral structure. According to the EDS mapping data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0001">1e–k</a>), the distribution of V, Zr, and Si confirms the presence of Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP. The aggregation of the reactant particles in the precursor solution during the fabrication of the sample may result in the nonuniformity of V in the Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0036_R_d92847927e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Energy">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>Although the Si signal interferes with the SiN<i><sub>x</sub></i> membrane of the in situ TEM chip, the distribution of the Si signal in the NZSP still has a stronger contrast. The electrochemical measurements performed by our group previously demonstrated that Na<sub>3</sub>VP possessed stable performance with slow capacity fading (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0035_R_d92847927e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At full desodiation, the value of the volume change of Na<sub>3</sub>VP is ≈8.8%, which is larger than that of Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (8.68%),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0037_R_d92847927e843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Power Sources">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>4</sub>MnCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (7.7%),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0038_R_d92847927e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>3.8</sub>MnV<sub>0.8</sub>Zr<sub>0.2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (4.9%).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0039_R_d92847927e871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The result is against previous study since the larger volume change usually leads to poor cyclability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0040_R_d92847927e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20430864-becb-464f-b303-6233b04e8396/advs6172-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/20430864-becb-464f-b303-6233b04e8396/advs6172-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b9f049f5-06dc-4d52-b111-05782574e74e/advs6172-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20430864-becb-464f-b303-6233b04e8396/advs6172-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Pristine state of the sample observed using TEM and EDS. a) TEM image of the sample. b) Schematic diagram showing the Na<sub>3</sub>VP electrode and the NZSP electrolyte. c,d) HRTEM images of Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP, respectively. EDS images of e) Na, f) V, g) Pt, h) Zr, i) Si, j) P, and k) O, respectively. The white dotted lines represent the interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To investigate the structural evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>VP owing to its close relationship with the electrochemical performance, the operando XRD experiment was conducted in this work. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> in the Supporting Information shows the cell charged/discharged between 2.7 and 4 V and the selected operando XRD patterns is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0002">2</a>. The Na<sub>3</sub>VP in R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/419314c2-2de3-4e8b-b865-c76d8a6de3a1/advs6172-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>c space group was detected before cycling. During the charge process, the intensity of the diffraction peak of Na<sub>3</sub>VP phase at 14.26° gradually decreased, and a weak reflection at 14.76° started to appear close to the mid-charge (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0002">2a</a>). In the meanwhile, the diffraction peak of Na<sub>3</sub>VP phase at 34.67° gradually disappeared, accompanying a weak reflection at 36.05° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0002">2b</a>). During the discharge process, the peaks at 14.76° and 36.05° disappeared with an increased intensity of the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>VP at 14.26° and 34.67°. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0002">2c</a> illustrates the intensity evolution of the diffraction peaks during charging/discharging processes at 14.26°, 14.76°, 34.67°, and 36.05°, respectively. Previous study reported that the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase appeared at 2<i>θ</i> ≈ 7.7° and 19.15° with the wavelength of incident X-ray being 0.8266 Å.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0041_R_d92847927e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the equation of Bragg's Law

            <div class="inline-equation" id="advs6172-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9bdbf5de-46e2-45f5-88de-c15ee00684c1/advs6172-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>n</mi>
                           <mi>λ</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </mrow>
                           <mi>d</mi>
                           <mi>sin</mi>
                           <mi>θ</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}n\lambda {\mathrm{ = 2}}d{\mathrm{sin}}\theta \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where the diffraction order <i>n</i> and the lattice spacing <i>d</i> are constant for a specific plane, the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase would appear at 14.37° and 36.12° with the wavelength of incident X-ray being 1.54 Å in our work. The calculated 2<i>θ</i> (14.37° and 36.12°) are closed to the diffraction peaks (14.76° and 36.05°) appearing in the mid-charge. It is suggested that the new reflections are attributed to the new intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ac544431-4227-4b73-a143-557a85421fb2/advs6172-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ac544431-4227-4b73-a143-557a85421fb2/advs6172-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b4ae4994-3bcc-49e8-a730-e167ee45f9c8/advs6172-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ac544431-4227-4b73-a143-557a85421fb2/advs6172-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Selected in situ XRD patterns of Na<sub>3</sub>VP during first cycle with a voltage window of 2.7–4.0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at the C-rate of 0.4 C. The narrower 2<i>θ</i> range are a) 13.6°–15.0° and b) 33.6°–36.4°. c) The intensity evolution of the diffraction peaks at 14.26°, 14.76°, 34.67°, and 36.05°, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further understand the Na-ion insertion/extraction mechanism, the desodiation process of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode is investigated via in situ HRTEM. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the TEM image of the sample desodiated to 4 V. The initial lattice spacings were 1.88 and 1.895 Å, corresponding to the (4<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7456aec9-455a-409c-bc62-1e1fa601af2f/advs6172-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0004.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>2</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{2}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>6) and (1<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3bb61d95-bdca-4ec6-9c8b-c1bbdfde8fcb/advs6172-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>5) planes of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP crystal. After the desodiation process, the lattice spacings narrowed to 1.835 and 1.84 Å, respectively, which correspond to the same lattice planes of NaVP with space group R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2b433048-3600-4acc-a6de-6ceeb8939abd/advs6172-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0006.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>c <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0042_R_d92847927e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Chem. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3b–d</a> and Movie <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> in the Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> in the Supporting Information shows the intensity line profiles of (4<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/df426e9a-d46f-4032-a56b-5061077d958d/advs6172-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0007.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>2</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{2}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>6) and (1<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/14919b51-564a-49e5-a45c-ed8d5b36050a/advs6172-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0008.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>5) upon desodiation. This result indicates that the lattice contracted upon the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3e</a> represents a particle of an intermediate phase, Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Na<sub>2</sub>VP), with a different crystal structure from Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaVP, which was observed outside the in situ observation area (inside the purple square in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3a</a>) after desodiating. This intermediate structure was consistent with a monoclinic structure with the P2<sub>1</sub>/c space group reported in a previous study.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0041_R_d92847927e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The values of unit cell volume per formula unit (<i>V</i>/<i>Z</i>) of Na<sub>3</sub>VP, Na<sub>2</sub>VP, and NaVP are 241.277,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0043_R_d92847927e1118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 227.159,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0041_R_d92847927e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 220.105 Å<sup>3</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0041_R_d92847927e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The calculated volume changes from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to Na<sub>2</sub>VP and from Na<sub>2</sub>VP to NaVP were 5.9% and 3.1%, respectively, while the volume change in the phase transition between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaVP was 8.8%.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e9caf04f-0ea4-4dd6-9dc1-ebf97c251f98/advs6172-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e9caf04f-0ea4-4dd6-9dc1-ebf97c251f98/advs6172-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7f8bb7e0-91f5-4788-9d9d-dcaf874642fd/advs6172-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e9caf04f-0ea4-4dd6-9dc1-ebf97c251f98/advs6172-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Desodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode. a) TEM image of the sample after desodiation. The green and purple squares indicate the regions inside and outside the in situ area, respectively. b–d) HRTEM images demonstrating the desodiation process of Na<sub>3</sub>VP. e) HRTEM image of the intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase. EDS images of f) Na, g) V, and h) Pt, respectively. The white dotted lines represent the interfaces between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Upon Na<sub>3</sub>VP desodiating to Na<sub>2</sub>VP, the average V–O bond length in VO<sub>6</sub> octahedron becomes longer, which contributes to the increase of the V–O octahedron volume. As Na<sub>2</sub>VP desodiates to NaVP, the V–O octahedron volume shrinks (as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> in the Supporting Information). It is suggested that although the value of <i>V</i>/<i>Z</i> gradually decreases with a decreasing number of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the larger VO<sub>6</sub> octahedron volume in Na<sub>2</sub>VP causes a slight expansion in [V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>3−</sup> framework. Therefore, the total volume changes from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to Na<sub>2</sub>VP and from Na<sub>2</sub>VP to NaVP (9.0%) is slightly larger than that from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to NaVP (8.8%). It is suggested that the gradual volume change, that is, from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to Na<sub>2</sub>VP and from Na<sub>2</sub>VP to NaVP, could reduce the lattice mismatch between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaVP, which will be further validated below. EDS mapping analysis of the desodiated sample showed that the Na signal of the cathode weakened after desodiation, indicating the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3f</a>). Without an anode, the diffused Na<sup>+</sup> ions through the NZSP accumulated around the Pt electrode (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3f,h</a>). The SAED images of the pristine and desodiated Pt electrodes showed the amorphousness of the electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the diffused Na<sup>+</sup> ions did not alloy with Pt. The immobility of V indicates the structural integrity of the [V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>3−</sup> framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>According to previous reports on layered sodium transition metal oxides, these materials undergo phase transitions owing to changes in the stacking sequence or the collapse of layered structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0044_R_d92847927e1255" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano Lett.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an example, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnO<sub>2</sub>-based oxides undergo detrimental phase transitions due to the impact of the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0045_R_d92847927e1268" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy Environ. Sci.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results indicate a correlation between structural evolution (increasing disorder in and among the layers) and performance degradation. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3e</a>, an intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase was observed, which also implies structural evolution during the desodiation process. However, the high stability and cycling ability of Na<sub>3</sub>VP has been demonstrated previously,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0046_R_d92847927e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which seems to contradict the studies mentioned above unless the occurring lattice distortions are reversible. Hence, an investigation of the phase transition between Na<sub>2</sub>VP and Na<sub>3</sub>VP is essential. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6172-fig-0004">4a–c</a> shows a series of HRTEM images of the phase transition from Na<sub>2</sub>VP to Na<sub>3</sub>VP, with the sample sodiated to 4 V. Initially, the lattice spacings were 2.542 and 2.992 Å, corresponding to the (60<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2534696c-1320-4633-983c-1330b5a8a8f3/advs6172-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0009.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>2</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{2}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) and (3<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d48a3391-906f-4a8c-a1ae-2511e7e225b8/advs6172-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0010" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0010.png">

The lattice spacings were 2.519 and 2.788 Å after sodiation for the (300) and (11<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/97571f90-6759-428d-bbe6-4cdceda3e867/advs6172-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0011" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0011.png">

Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) shows the intensity line profiles of (60<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b3161fb8-0da0-4fc7-b0c0-957f1226cab5/advs6172-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0012" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0012.png">

               </semantics></math>) ((300)) and (3<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7f110672-a3c5-465d-80c7-2d18a611dfd9/advs6172-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0013" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0013.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>2</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>

                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{2}\bar{2}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) ((11<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5f8b2620-4d93-45d4-abc2-0e7194161e81/advs6172-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0014" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0014.png">

                     <mo>¯</mo>
