The EDS mapping of the sodiated sample showed that the Na signal became stronger (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0004">4d</a>), whereas the Na signal around the Pt electrode was weakened (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0004">4d,f</a>). This indicates that the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffused from NZSP toward cathode during sodiation. The distribution of V remained unchanged after sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0004">4e</a>), further confirming the structural stability of Na<sub>2</sub>VP/Na<sub>3</sub>VP.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca740183-3588-46ea-9843-a14ce8c7e65f/advs6172-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca740183-3588-46ea-9843-a14ce8c7e65f/advs6172-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0914348-fc07-4a75-9c2d-9f827f7f6342/advs6172-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca740183-3588-46ea-9843-a14ce8c7e65f/advs6172-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodiation of Na<sub>2</sub>VP. a–c) HRTEM images demonstrating the sodiation process of Na<sub>2</sub>VP. EDS images of d) Na, e) V, and f) Pt, respectively. The white dotted lines represent the interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The solid–solid contact between the electrode and electrolyte has been a crucial issue for all-solid-state batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0047_R_d92847927e1413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Microsc. Microanal.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The interfacial stress induced by the volume changes of active materials during sodiation/desodiation ruptures the interface between the electrolyte and electrode. As the cracks at the contacted interface form, the cross-section of the interface decreases, increasing the impedance of the interface. The increasing impedance causes more Joule heating, leading to a vicious cycle. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> in the Supporting Information, there was no visible crack formation at the interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP after one cycle. To verify the continuity of the interface, the resistances of the sample were measured under a constant bias of 4 V for 15 min. The resistance showed no obvious increase during desodiation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information), and sodiation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the contact between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP remained intact after cycling. Notably, the resistances decreased slightly and then increased in both the desodiated and sodiated curves (as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c,d</a>, respectively, Supporting Information). To clarify the effect of intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase on the interface, an in-depth experiment has been conducted. The sample was sodiated/desodiated to 4 V for three cycles. As the magnification becomes higher, the electron current density (dose rate) becomes larger, causing the structural degradation of cathode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0048_R_d92847927e1437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To avoid this situation, the in situ area with constant electron beam irradiation (to avoid the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>VP) was observed under relative low magnification during applying bias. After reaching 4 V, the interface was further observed with higher magnification. After three cycles, the interface outside the in situ area (with Na<sub>2</sub>VP) remained continue (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information) while the voids started to form along the interface within in situ area (without Na<sub>2</sub>VP) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information). It is suggested that the larger volume change between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaVP causes severer structural collapse than that from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to Na<sub>2</sub>VP and from Na<sub>2</sub>VP to NaVP. The lattice mismatch at the boundary of the intact and degraded phases cause the lattice strain that eventually leads to void formation during repeated charge/discharge.</p>
         
         <p>To further validate the sodiation/desodiation reactions of Na<sub>3</sub>VP, the SAED patterns of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP were analyzed at a constant voltage of 4 V for 15 min. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> in the Supporting Information shows a series of SAED images of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP during desodiation. The spectrum in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0005">5a</a> in the Supporting Information depicts the intensity from the transmitted beams to the diffraction spots (marked by the green arrows in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> in the Supporting Information). The bright diffraction spot has a high intensity because the intensity is determined by the brightness contrast of the SAED image. Therefore, the diffraction spot in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> in the Supporting Information forms a peak in the spectrum of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0005">5a</a> in the Supporting Information. The peak shifted slightly toward the right during the desodiation process, indicating an increase in the distance between the transmitted beam and the diffraction spot. In the reciprocal space, the distance from a diffraction spot to the transmitted beam is proportional to the reciprocal of the planar space. Therefore, it is suggested that the lattice spacing of Na<sub>3</sub>VP decreased when the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were extracted. This was consistent with the in situ TEM results. Na<sub>3</sub>VP was then sodiated for 15 min, and the corresponding SAED images are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> in the Supporting Information. The left shift of the peak (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0005">5b</a>) shows a decrease in the distance between the transmitted beam and the diffraction spot in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> in the Supporting Information. In other words, the lattice spacing of Na<sub>3</sub>VP expanded during the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/279182d1-6589-4330-a1d7-7b771919249c/advs6172-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/279182d1-6589-4330-a1d7-7b771919249c/advs6172-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f3030afc-bc90-468a-8f3c-3a6075d4eed6/advs6172-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/279182d1-6589-4330-a1d7-7b771919249c/advs6172-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of the sodiation/desodiation reaction of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode. The distances between the transmitted beams and the diffraction spots during a) desodiation and b) sodiation. The black dashed lines represent perpendicular lines.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition to the reversibility of the sodiation/desodiation process as confirmed via the in situ TEM and SAED patterns, rechargeability is also an important quality for a secondary battery. For the TEM sample fabricated by FIB, the sample has a lamellar shape and a thickness of ≈100 nm. The lamellar shape has a much smaller cross-sectional area compared with the bulk Na-ASSB, resulting in a higher current density. Moreover, contacts of Pt wires and the sample (as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a> in the Supporting Information), where the current direction and current density change, suffer from the current crowding effect. Therefore, thermal stresses could affect the in situ TEM sample significantly, which may deteriorate its rechargeability. To minimize the thermal impact on the samples, Pt pads with a side length of 1.5 µm were deposited on the contacts via FIB (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b–d</a>, Supporting Information). The Pt pads were expected to increase the contact area between the Pt wires and the sample and reduce the change in the current direction. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the STEM images and Na mapping of the sample from the first to the third cycle. The changes in the intensity of the Na signal in Na<sub>3</sub>VP verified the release/uptake of Na<sup>+</sup> ions during the desodiation/sodiation process, while the unchanged V distribution (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information) and the corresponding HRTEM images of Na<sub>3</sub>VP after cycling (from the first to the third cycle) shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> in the Supporting Information validated the stability of the NASICON structure. After cycling, the interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NSZP still exhibited excellent contact. It is suggested that the optimization of the in situ device mitigated the thermal effect on the sample, improving its rechargeability. EELS was utilized to investigate the valence states of V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0006">6b</a> shows the V-L<sub>2,3</sub> spectra of pristine, desodiated, and sodiated Na<sub>3</sub>VP. The L-edge shifted toward higher energies due to Na<sup>+</sup> ion extraction. The valence state of V changed from +3 to +4, which corresponds to the desodiation from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to NaVP. The L-edge shifted toward lower energies when the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were inserted, suggesting the recurrence of Na<sub>3</sub>VP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0049_R_d92847927e1582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/089f7cb8-b78d-44ed-8713-859857a9ee37/advs6172-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/089f7cb8-b78d-44ed-8713-859857a9ee37/advs6172-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca97aa6e-0cfc-4e39-9ca5-a079686e3be9/advs6172-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/089f7cb8-b78d-44ed-8713-859857a9ee37/advs6172-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">EDS mapping and EELS spectra of Na<sub>3</sub>VP. a) The STEM image of the sample and their corresponding EDS mapping of Na during three cycles. The white dotted lines represent the interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP. b) EELS spectra of the V L-edge of the pristine, desodiated, and sodiated states of Na<sub>3</sub>VP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>For a better understanding of the mechanism of the phase transition in the Na<sub>3</sub>VP–NaVP system, an in-depth discussion of the structural transformation is necessary. Each Na<sub>3</sub>VP structural unit can accommodate three Na atoms, where one is located at the Na(1) site and the other two are located at the Na(2) sites. The Na(2) ions are considered to be the main participants in the transportation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions because of their longer Na–O distance than that of the Na(1) site. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6172-fig-0007">7a–c</a> shows the structural evolution after the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were extracted. The lantern unit consists of two VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra sharing corners with three PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. The yellow and blue spheres represent Na<sup>+</sup> ions at the Na(1) and Na(2) sites, respectively. In the rhombohedral Na<sub>3</sub>VP, both Na(1) and Na(2) sites are partially occupied (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0007">7a</a>). During the desodiation process, Na<sup>+</sup> ions are extracted from the Na(2) sites, resulting in less occupancy of this position. The extraction of the Na(2) ions reduces the rhombohedral symmetry due to a distortion of the lattice, transforming the rhombohedral structure into a monoclinic structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0007">7b</a>). Once all Na<sup>+</sup> ions are extracted from the Na(2) sites, the monoclinic structure then transforms back to the rhombohedral phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0007">7c</a>), owing to the symmetry of the vacant Na(2) sites. Furthermore, DFT computations were employed to determine the formation energies of the NaVP crystals along the Na<sub>3</sub>VP and Na<sub>2</sub>VP pathways. Among the different structures, Na<sub>3</sub>VP has the highest formation energy (−2.229 eV per atom), while Na<sub>2</sub>VP and NaVP manifest lower formation energies of −2.264 and −2.280 eV per atom, respectively. This indicates that the Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode prefers to transform to the Na<sub>2</sub>VP or NaVP structures. Moreover, the desodiation energy from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to Na<sub>2</sub>VP is much lower than that from Na<sub>3</sub>VP to NaVP, demonstrating that the Na<sub>3</sub>VP structure is more likely to transform to Na<sub>2</sub>VP structure during the desodiation process (path A in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> in the Supporting Information) rather than the NaVP structure (path B). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> in the Supporting Information shows the diffusion barriers for the Na<sup>+</sup> ion migration in the Na<sub>2</sub>VP and Na<sub>3</sub>VP structures. The energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in the Na<sub>2</sub>VP crystal is 0.138 eV, which is smaller than that in Na<sub>3</sub>VP (0.298 eV). For NaVP, where all Na ions only occupy Na(1) sites, the extraction of Na ions may affect the structural integrity. Therefore, Na migration between Na(1) sites at NaVP is hindered by a higher migration barrier than Na<sub>3</sub>VP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0050_R_d92847927e1698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Mater. Chem. A">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggests that the Na<sup>+</sup> ion migrates more rapidly in the Na<sub>2</sub>VP structure compared to Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaVP, thereby decreasing the resistance of the sample (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c,d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs6172-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d113aa0-6a93-4543-a983-0587f60564c2/advs6172-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d113aa0-6a93-4543-a983-0587f60564c2/advs6172-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/741fe010-b1fe-461b-a8dd-23bcdb0cc97b/advs6172-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d113aa0-6a93-4543-a983-0587f60564c2/advs6172-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6172-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Reaction mechanism of Na<sub>3</sub>VP cathode. The lantern unit and the occupancy of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in a) Na<sub>3</sub>VP, b) Na<sub>2</sub>VP, and c) NaVP. d) Schematic diagram showing the evolution of structure during the sodiation/desodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>VP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on the results of the in situ TEM, Na<sub>3</sub>VP can transform to NaVP with or without the Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase. This behavior could be attributed to electron beam irradiation. As mentioned above, high magnification of TEM could cause the degradation of cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0048_R_d92847927e1749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The state of the aperture affects not only the number of incident electrons on the sample, but it also affects the area of irradiation. Therefore, the electron current density (dose rate) remain the same in spite of the state of the aperture. Although the electron irradiation of TEM may cause irreversible damage to the sample,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0051_R_d92847927e1754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it can provide a feasible stimulus that mimics the effect of electrochemical cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0052_R_d92847927e1760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Huang et&nbsp;al. reported that electron beams could reclaim Li metal from the degradation product and serve as a local Li source, which triggers the lithiation of NiFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/carbon nanotubes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0053_R_d92847927e1770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Mater. Chem. A">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> LiNi<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Co<sub>0.18</sub>Ti<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> particles, repeated electron beam irradiation induced a phase transition from an R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/255fa0fb-39a0-4593-8808-c7c3ed39be62/advs6172-math-0015.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0015" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0015.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>m layered structure to an Fm<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/54a65726-48c6-462e-a1d4-dfd80a94116d/advs6172-math-0016.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0016" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0016.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>m rock-salt structure, which is attributed to the stoichiometric lithium and oxygen removal from R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/676ceee5-b8e4-4826-a133-3de89fe7ed97/advs6172-math-0017.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs6172:advs6172-math-0017" display="inline" location="graphic/advs6172-math-0017.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>m 3a and 6c sites, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0054_R_d92847927e1810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Sci. Rep.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This implies that the electron beam can be used as a high-energy particle flow to change the microstructure of sensitive materials. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3b–d</a>, the Na<sub>3</sub>VP particle was exposed to a high-energy electron beam throughout the entire desodiation process. Na<sup>+</sup> ions could be easily extracted from Na<sub>3</sub>VP owing to the interaction between electrons and atoms. Therefore, the Na<sub>3</sub>VP phase could transform to the NaVP phase directly without the formation of the intermediary Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase (path B in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0007">7d</a>). Meanwhile, the Na<sub>3</sub>VP outside the in situ observation region, where the effect of electron beam irradiation was insignificant, transformed to Na<sub>2</sub>VP, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0003">3e</a>, thus lowering the formation energy of NaVP (path A in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6172-fig-0007">7d</a>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6172-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6172-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a Na<sub>3</sub>VP/NZSP cell with excellent solid–solid contact was fabricated. The charging/discharging process of Na<sub>3</sub>VP was investigated using operando XRD. The structural evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>VP was demonstrated with in situ TEM. The lattice spacing decreased during Na<sup>+</sup> ion extraction, which corresponds to the transformation of Na<sub>3</sub>VP to NaVP. Outside the in situ area, the intermediary Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase with the space group P2<sub>1</sub>/c was observed at atomic scale. Na<sub>2</sub>VP could reduce the lattice mismatch between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NaVP, thereby preventing structural collapse. Therefore, the interface between Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP remained intact with the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase. The mechanism of the phase transition from Na<sub>2</sub>VP to Na<sub>3</sub>VP was revealed by analyzing the structural evolution during sodiation. In addition to the lattice spacing, the dihedral angle was also altered when the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were inserted. The formation of the intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase can be attributed to the asymmetry of Na(2) sites in the rhombohedral structure. The Na<sub>2</sub>VP phase lowers the formation energy of NaVP and possesses lower energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. This study allows a better understand of the behavior of fast-rate electrode materials that undergoes phase transition, providing insight into the future design of Na<sub>3</sub>VP.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6172-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6172-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6172-sec-0070-title"> Na<sub>3</sub>VP and NZSP Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>NZSP powder was synthesized on a laboratory scale (10 g<sup>−1</sup> kg per batch) using a solution-assisted solid-state reaction method (SA-SSR).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0006_R_d92847927e1913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ChemElectroChem">6</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0055_R_d92847927e1913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Mater. Chem. A">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaNO<sub>3</sub> (VWR), ZrO(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (Aldrich), Si(OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub> (Merck), and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (Merck) were used as starting materials. All the chemicals applied in this study were analytically pure. Stoichiometric amounts of NaNO<sub>3</sub> and ZrO(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> were dissolved in deionized water. A stoichiometric amount of Si(OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub> was also added to the solution while stirring. When Si(OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub> was hydrolyzed, the stoichiometric amount of NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> was added to the system during stirring. The homogeneous aqueous solution immediately showed formation of complex zirconium oxyphosphate compounds. The whole mixture was dried at 85&nbsp;°C. The dried powder was then calcined at 800&nbsp;°C for 3 h. After calcination, a white powder was obtained. The calcined powder was then milled in ethanol with zirconia balls on a milling bench for 48 h, and dried at 70&nbsp;°C for 12 h.</p>
                  
                  <p>The NZSP powder was put into a cylindrical pressing mold (13 mm in diameter) and pressed with a uniaxial pressure of about 100 MPa at room temperature. The pressed pellets were sintered between 1260 and 1300&nbsp;°C for 5 h. The obtained pellets had a diameter of about 10 mm, and thickness of 1–2 mm. The relative density of the sintered pellets was &gt;95%.</p>
                  
                  <p>The Na<sub>3</sub>VP precursor solution was prepared by mixing ethanolamine, de-ionized H<sub>2</sub>O, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> (weight ratios of 1:2:0.71:0.46). A cotton swab was dipped into the Na<sub>3</sub>VP precursor solution and then swept over the surface of the sintered NZSP pellet, leaving a thin and homogenous Na<sub>3</sub>VP precursor solution layer on the NZSP pellet. The pellets were then heated to 740&nbsp;°C in Ar-2% H<sub>2</sub> for 4 h to form the Na<sub>3</sub>VP phase on the NZSP surface.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6172-sec-0080-title"> Operando XRD Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>For the operando XRD analysis, Au was then sputtered on the positive electrodes to serve as current collector. The cell was assembled inside Ar-filled glove box, subjected into X-ray examination during charging/discharging at a rate of 0.4 C. Bruker D2 Phaser and BioLogic SP-150 Potentiostat are used as X-ray diffraction instrument and a modulo-battery-mode electrochemical tester, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6172-sec-0090-title"> In Situ TEM Observation</h4>
                  
                  <p>An in situ TEM sample of Na<sub>3</sub>VP/NZSP was prepared using a high-resolution dual-beam focused ion beam (FIB) system (FEI Versa 3D). Because of the unevenness of the Na<sub>3</sub>VP/NZSP sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information), a Pt protection layer was deposited first to smoothen the surface using the FIB technique. The second Pt layer was then deposited onto the first Pt layer to prevent damage from the Ga ions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>, Supporting Information). The trenches were milled on both sides of the lamella, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18c</a> in the Supporting Information. A low-kV cleaning process was used at the end of the FIB process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20d</a>, Supporting Information) to reduce the influence of Ga ions. The sample was then transferred by a glass tip onto the in situ TEM chip, and Pt wires were deposited by the FIB system to connect the electrodes of the chip to those of the TEM sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20e</a>, Supporting Information). An enlarged scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of the TEM sample is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20f</a> in the Supporting Information. Subsequently, the chip was mounted onto an in situ TEM holder (Protochips Audro300). In situ TEM movies and images were recorded using a JEOL F200 system equipped with EDS using OneView CCD.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6172-sec-0100-title"> Characterization of Pristine/Sodiated/Desodiated Na<sub>3</sub>VP</h4>
                  
                  <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of the pristine sample was measured using a Brucker D2 Phaser with Cu Kα radiation. After the samples were sodiated/desodiated to 4 V, the valence states of V in Na<sub>3</sub>VP were characterized using EELS.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6172-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6172-sec-0110-title"> Density Functional Theory Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>First-principles calculations were conducted by using the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0056_R_d92847927e2052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">56</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0057_R_d92847927e2052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Phys. Rev. B">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the projector augmented wave (PAW)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0058_R_d92847927e2057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Phys. Rev. B">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> method based on the density functional theory (DFT). The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0059_R_d92847927e2062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Phys. Rev. B">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> functional within the generalized-gradient approximation (GGA)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0060_R_d92847927e2068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used to describe the exchange-correlation interaction for the structure optimization. The cut-off energy was set to 600 eV for all the calculations. The first Brillouin zone was sampled with 3 × 3 × 1, 2 × 3 × 3, and 3 × 3 × 1 <i>k</i>-points grids for Na<sub>3</sub>VP, Na<sub>2</sub>VP, and NaVP crystals, respectively. The energy convergence criterion was set as 1.0 × 10<sup>−5</sup> for all the calculations. The optimized lattice parameters (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.832 Å, <i>c</i> = 22.094 Å for Na<sub>3</sub>VP; <i>a</i> = 15.432 Å, <i>b</i> = 8.718 Å, <i>c</i> = 8.851 Å for Na<sub>2</sub>VP; <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.496 Å, <i>c</i> = 21.656 Å for NaVP) are good consistent with the experimental values.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0035_R_d92847927e2106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0041_R_d92847927e2106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>

                  <div class="paragraph-element">The desodiation energy (<i>E</i><sub>D</sub>) of the NaVP crystal during the desodiation process is expressed by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#advs6172-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>)

                        <semantics>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>E</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>Na</mi>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi>x</mi>
                                       <mo>+</mo>
                                       <mi>y</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </msub>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>V</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msub>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>PO</mi>
                                          <mn>4</mn>
                                       </msub>
                                       <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mn>3</mn>
                                 </msub>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${E}_{{\mathrm{Na}}_{x + y}{V}_2{{( {{\mathrm{PO}}_4} )}}_3}$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math> is the total energy of the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i><sub>+</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>VP crystals per formula units (<i>x</i> = 1, 2, 3; <i>y</i> = 1, 2)</div>
                  
                  <p>The transition-state (TS) was determined using the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) calculation through the VTST code<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6172-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0061_R_d92847927e2315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Chem. Phys.">61</a>, <a href="#advs6172-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6172-bib-0062_R_d92847927e2315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Chem. Phys.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the diffusion barriers related to the energy difference between the initial-state (IS) and the TS. All atoms were allowed to relax until the force components were less than 0.05 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
